    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app',

<span id="freeTextContainer16264555020481968718"><i>The Book of the New Sun</i> is unanimously acclaimed as Gene Wolfe's most remarkable work, hailed as "a masterpiece of science fantasy comparable in importance to the major works of Tolkien and Lewis" by <i>Publishers Weekly</i>, and "one of the most ambitious works of speculative fiction in the twentieth century" by <i>The Magazine of Fantasy and Science Fiction</i>. <i>Sword & Citadel </i>brings</span>
  <span id="freeText16264555020481968718" style="display:none"><i>The Book of the New Sun</i> is unanimously acclaimed as Gene Wolfe's most remarkable work, hailed as "a masterpiece of science fantasy comparable in importance to the major works of Tolkien and Lewis" by <i>Publishers Weekly</i>, and "one of the most ambitious works of speculative fiction in the twentieth century" by <i>The Magazine of Fantasy and Science Fiction</i>. <i>Sword &amp; Citadel </i>brings together the final two books of the tetralogy in one volume:<br /><br /><i>The Sword of the Lictor</i> is the third volume in Wolfe's remarkable epic, chronicling the odyssey of the wandering pilgrim called Severian, driven by a powerful and unfathomable destiny, as he carries out a dark mission far from his home.<br /><br /><i>The Citadel of the Autarch</i> brings The Book of the New Sun to its harrowing conclusion, as Severian clashes in a final reckoning with the dread Autarch, fulfilling an ancient prophecy that will forever alter the realm known as Urth.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16264555020481968718" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_148187780" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/148187780" />
    <a title="Dan Schwent" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/870755-dan-schwent"><img alt="Dan Schwent" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593973948p2/870755.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/148187780?book_show_action=true">Feb 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan Schwent" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan Schwent" href="/user/show/870755-dan-schwent">Dan Schwent</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=2011">2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=sf">sf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer148187780" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14933674256865393461"><b>Sword of the Lictor:</b> Severian's stay in Thrax is short lived. After helping a woman escape instead of strangling her, Severian flees Thrax to look for the Pelerines. But can he find them before trouble finds him...?<br/><br/>The plot of the Book of the New Sun progresses quite a bit in this volume. I don't want to give too much away but Severian sure doesn't stay in Thrax very long. I'm still not precisely sure what the hell is going on but it's a pretty enjoyable read. Wolfe's prose has to be savored, no</span>
  <span id="freeText14933674256865393461" style="display:none"><b>Sword of the Lictor:</b> Severian's stay in Thrax is short lived. After helping a woman escape instead of strangling her, Severian flees Thrax to look for the Pelerines. But can he find them before trouble finds him...?<br /><br />The plot of the Book of the New Sun progresses quite a bit in this volume. I don't want to give too much away but Severian sure doesn't stay in Thrax very long. I'm still not precisely sure what the hell is going on but it's a pretty enjoyable read. Wolfe's prose has to be savored, not scarfed. I've read before that Gene Wolfe's biggest influence is G.K. Chesterton. The deeper into the Book of the New Sun I get, the more I get flashes of Chesterton. Parts of The Book of the New Sun definitely have a The Man Who Was Thursday quality to them.<br /><br />My favorite part was the relationship between Big Severian and Little Severian. The retelling of the story of Romulus and Remus and the return of some characters were nice touches. Dorcas' origin was finally revealed and was as I suspected when she first entered the tale.<br /><br />Severian's grown as a character since the first book and I eagerly await his rise to Autarch in the final volume.<br /><br /><b>Citadel of the Autarch:</b> Severian finally reaches the camp of the Pelerines, goes to war, and finally meets his destiny.<br /><br />A lot happened in The Citadel of the Autarch. Severian has a variety of adventures and meets up with most of the rest of the cast for a final time, and becomes the Autarch, as he said he would early on in the first book. It felt more like a Dying Earth story than the earlier ones and was probably the easiest to read of the four. Questions were answered, but not all of them. Extra points were scored when Arioch was mentioned on the first page.<br /><br />The writing in Citadel of the Autarch was much the same as in the other volumes but I detected echoes of Michael Moorcock in addition to the usual Jack Vance and G.K. Chesterton. And like Chesterton, I like that Wolfe left some aspects open to interpretation. Much like the reveals of which of the characters are policemen rather anarchists in The Man Who Was Thursday, I eventually was thinking "How many characters were actually the Autarch or working for him?"<br /><br />While it's not a quick read, I found my mind returning to the Book of the New Sun again and again. I'm sure I'll be re-reading it again in the future.<br /><br /><b>2012 Note:</b> I went ahead and bumped this up to a 5. I'm not sure if it deserves it but I still catch myself thinking about this book almost a year after I finished it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14933674256865393461" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_247376345" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/247376345" />
    <a title="Traveller" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3145526-traveller"><img alt="Traveller" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1423246742p2/3145526.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/247376345?book_show_action=true">Dec 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Traveller" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Traveller" href="/user/show/3145526-traveller">Traveller</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9780283992162" href="/book/show/1350750.The_Sword_of_the_Lictor_and_the_Citadel_of_the_Autarch">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3145526-traveller?shelf=alternate-world">alternate-world</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3145526-traveller?shelf=sf">sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3145526-traveller?shelf=po-mo">po-mo</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer247376345" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13325493468134669683">Hmmm, the only reason I'm not giving this a 5, is that the fourth book in the series becomes almost too esoteric, even for me, a lover of esotericism, to digest.<br/><br/>I haven't read the Urth of the New Sun, the 5th book that is supposed to clear everything up, yet. I might revise my opinion once I've done that, but... let's just say my judgment has become a bit clouded by all the commentary I've by now read on the series and the religious flavorings that such extraneous commentary lends to the work. <br/><br/>I</span>
  <span id="freeText13325493468134669683" style="display:none">Hmmm, the only reason I'm not giving this a 5, is that the fourth book in the series becomes almost too esoteric, even for me, a lover of esotericism, to digest.<br /><br />I haven't read the Urth of the New Sun, the 5th book that is supposed to clear everything up, yet. I might revise my opinion once I've done that, but... let's just say my judgment has become a bit clouded by all the commentary I've by now read on the series and the religious flavorings that such extraneous commentary lends to the work. <br /><br />I suppose that seeing The Lion Witch and Wardrobe series as being a Christian allegory now, didn't make me like that series any the less when I read it as a child; so why should the "New Sun" saga be diminished by something similar? <br /><br />I'm not saying that it is thus diminished; the work is certainly too rich, dense and sophisticated for it to be a mere attempt at proselytisation, and one can certainly enjoy the narrative at many levels.<br /><br />However, I think I might have to read the 5th book and then perhaps re-read some of the earlier books yet again, to get a better 'grip' on all that is being said and suggested.<br /><br /><br />This is not an easy body of work to wrestle to the ground. For those looking for hackneyed bodice-rippers, you will be disappointed, I fear. If you are looking for something that is original, and at times weird and thought-provoking, this is it.<br /><br />New Sun often has one going: "Hey, wait a minute...."<br /><br />Oh, and a warning to the wise; it is essential that you read the books belonging to the series in order, starting with the Shadow of the Torturer; more than any other series I have ever read, (perhaps even more so than even LOTR).<br /><br /> The first 4 books of this series should be read as if they are chapters of one book, because they are all indispensable to one another; you cannot really judge any of them on their own merit (though to me SOTT was probably the most satisfying as a standalone out of all 4)<br /><br />I also think that the series was written with one of it's goals being that it should be re-read. Severian even says at the very end: "Have I told you all I promised? I am aware that at various places in<br />my narrative I have pledged that this or that should be made clear in<br />the knitting up of the story. I remember them all, I am sure, but then<br />I remember so much else.<b> Before you assume that I have cheated<br />you, read again.</b>"<br /><br />It is in this volume that the postmodernist character of the New Sun cycle most clearly comes into focus, with so many dualities and pluralities and facets breaking into a rainbow of views that deconstruct it's own origin that it makes your head spin.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13325493468134669683" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2476847091" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2476847091" />
    <a title="Nick Imrie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5906293-nick-imrie"><img alt="Nick Imrie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591436280p2/5906293.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2476847091?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nick Imrie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nick Imrie" href="/user/show/5906293-nick-imrie">Nick Imrie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5906293-nick-imrie?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5906293-nick-imrie?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5906293-nick-imrie?shelf=sf-time-travel">sf-time-travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5906293-nick-imrie?shelf=fantasy-doorstopper">fantasy-doorstopper</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5906293-nick-imrie?shelf=fantasy-high">fantasy-high</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5906293-nick-imrie?shelf=unreliable-narrator">unreliable-narrator</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5906293-nick-imrie?shelf=award-british-fantasy">award-british-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5906293-nick-imrie?shelf=award-locus-fantasy">award-locus-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5906293-nick-imrie?shelf=award-campbell-memorial">award-campbell-memorial</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2476847091" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1373993554985876385">This review is for the whole thing, The Book of the New Sun Volumes 1 -4, including <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40992.Shadow___Claw__The_Book_of_the_New_Sun__1_2_' title='Shadow & Claw (The Book of the New Sun #1-2) by Gene Wolfe' rel='nofollow noopener'>Shadow & Claw</a>, because, really, it is one big story.<br/><br/>Now I realise that for many people this is not even close to being the most important consideration, but I have to address the question: is it Fantasy or is it Science Fiction?<br/>I'm a sucker for classifying, categorising, and defining, so this is the kind of question I take great pleasure in. If you don't, feel free to skip on down the paragraphs.<br/><br/>I'm nailing my co</span>
  <span id="freeText1373993554985876385" style="display:none">This review is for the whole thing, The Book of the New Sun Volumes 1 -4, including <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40992.Shadow___Claw__The_Book_of_the_New_Sun__1_2_" title="Shadow &amp; Claw (The Book of the New Sun #1-2) by Gene Wolfe" rel="nofollow noopener">Shadow &amp; Claw</a>, because, really, it is one big story.<br /><br />Now I realise that for many people this is not even close to being the most important consideration, but I have to address the question: is it Fantasy or is it Science Fiction?<br />I'm a sucker for classifying, categorising, and defining, so this is the kind of question I take great pleasure in. If you don't, feel free to skip on down the paragraphs.<br /><br />I'm nailing my colours to the mast: it's definitely fantasy.<br /><br />Now a lot of people say it's science fiction. At first the story appears to be set in your typical fantasy cod-medieval Europe. The sort of setting well-beloved in the fantasy genre: witches, emperors, swords, spears, horses, whores, men with muscular bared chests. But a little attention soon shows that it's actually a far-future society: feudal people living in the ruins of a formerly advanced civilisation. This isn't part of the Twist. It's clear very early when Severian describes the Matachin Tower, mentioning that some of the rooms were formerly 'fuel chambers' and making it clear in other ways that the 'tower' is actually a rocket. Then it becomes a fun game to figure out when Severian is seeing something that we would recognise as high tech, but he just sees as so much ancient rubble. So it's a science fiction setting, disguised as a fantasy setting - and as the story progresses various science fictional aspects come into play.<br /><br />But I say it's fantasy, because for me, an SF setting isn't enough to make something science fiction. Aliens, time travel, spaceships, wormholes - these are the set dressing of science fiction, but not the heart of it. The heart of SF is its subject matter. The <a href="http://www.sf-encyclopedia.com/entry/novum" rel="nofollow noopener">novum</a>, the <a href="" rel="nofollow noopener">sensawunda</a>, rational man conquering his environment through applied intelligence.<br />The Book of the New Sun isn't really concerned with any of these things. The SFnal tropes in this book all serve fantasy purposes: they are tools in that classic fantasy plot: The Quest. Severian's journey is primarily an internal journey, a moral journey to become a man and do the right thing. This is the heart of fantasy. And that's why this is fundamentally a fantasy story.<br /><br />I love this book. I love the intricate, archaic language and the strangely familiar neologisms. I love Severian and all his neurotic monologuing. I loved the old men of the Matachin Tower. God help me, I even loved <i>Terminus Est</i>. This affection crept up on me so slowly that I didn't even know I was feeling it, until the fateful fight with Baldaners left me heart-broken.<br /><br />Lucky me. Because there are many people who find these books something of a chore, redeemed (or not) by The Twist. The Twist (without wanting to give too much away in a goodreads review) is something that becomes apparent in the final 10% of the story, where certain information throws many earlier events into an entirely new light. Now, I enjoyed the story and I enjoyed the twist, so it's all good for me. But I have a lot of sympathy for those who did not enjoy the story, and felt that they had to wade through quite a lot of typical fantasy trash, tarted up in pompous prose, to get rewarded only at the very end. YMMV. The slow meandering story, the long digressions, the seemingly pointless diversions, are a joy for anyone who takes pleasure in world-building for world-building's sake.<br /><br />I'm also a sucker for an unreliable narrator. Here's another aspect that makes The Book of the New Sun such a rich source for endless talking over with friends. Just how unreliable is Severian? Many call him a liar, and many others have defied them to point out anywhere in the text where Severian tells a straight-up lie. It can't be done, he's never explicitly dishonest, but, boy, is he the master of misdirection and subtle elisions. The description of the avern duel just doesn't make any sense until 1000 pages later when we find out what Severian omitted in his first description.<br /><br />The richness of the world, the many puzzles and clues through-out the text, the heavy language, the huge dumps of philosophy and theology, The Twist, the inevitability of it all: these things make it book worth reading and rereading many times. But alas, only for those of us who enjoy the basic plot and characters in the first place.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1373993554985876385" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_77714415" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/77714415" />
    <a title="Szplug" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1843184-szplug"><img alt="Szplug" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1395686229p2/1843184.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/77714415?book_show_action=true">Nov 13, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Szplug" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Szplug" href="/user/show/1843184-szplug">Szplug</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer77714415" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9122816075659472662">Gene Wolfe, the poetically accented writer of intricate fantasy/science-fiction hybrids like this exquisite tetralogy, was inspired by that other pen-wielding magician Jack Vance's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40866.Tales_of_the_Dying_Earth' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <i>Tales of the Dying Earth</i>
</a>: Wolfe's series also takes place on a radically altered Earth in the far, far future when the Sun's fuel is running dangerously low. Amidst the wreckage of past civilizations lies the sprawling, endless city of Wolfe's protagonist torturer-apprentice Severian. Beginning as a gauzy, hauntin</span>
  <span id="freeText9122816075659472662" style="display:none">Gene Wolfe, the poetically accented writer of intricate fantasy/science-fiction hybrids like this exquisite tetralogy, was inspired by that other pen-wielding magician Jack Vance's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40866.Tales_of_the_Dying_Earth" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Tales of the Dying Earth</i>
</a>: Wolfe's series also takes place on a radically altered Earth in the far, far future when the Sun's fuel is running dangerously low. Amidst the wreckage of past civilizations lies the sprawling, endless city of Wolfe's protagonist torturer-apprentice Severian. Beginning as a gauzy, haunting <i>bildungsroman</i>, influenced subtly throughout by the Roman Catholic faith of the author and homages to other great works, Severian, cursed and blessed with a flawless memory that may or may not be allergic to the truth, makes a moral choice that leads to his flight from the vast metropolis, a journey requiring literally days of travel. As the story progresses while the fuligin-clad youth ventures forth past the city walls into unknown lands - and companions attach themselves to Severian in pursuit of unknown ends - the itinerant torturer becomes aware of troubles at the court of the Autarch, the all-powerful, myth-shrouded monarch of the torturer's world, who faces scheming rebel courtiers and consults with the enigmatic Hierodules. Severian will also hear rumors of a fierce war with strange-speaking foreigners from the alien culture of the north.<br /><br />Each of the four books that comprise the tetralogy unfold with a subtle shift in tone and tincture. In my opinion, the opening book possesses itself of an atmospheric wonder rarely achieved in the field of fantasy fiction. Whereas Tolkien's <i>The Fellowship of the Ring</i> begins with a localized and bucolic expedition that little hints at the historic importance of the quest vaguely discernible upon the horizon, and Stephen Donaldson's <i>Lord Foul's Bane</i> initiates the reader into the grim, isolated and self-loathing existence of an embittered leper whose first overwhelming experience with The Land inspires him to an act of rape that jars the reader out of the moment, Wolfe's <i>The Shadow of the Torturer</i> is, in many ways, the strongest book in the series. From the opening sentences the reader is immersed in the portentous but ineffable shimmering of a mysterious civilization aged in its incantatory urban flavor like an oaken whiskey; the entirety a smoothly flowing, warming, and immensely enjoyable unfolding as the words daub every action, puzzle, and rumination with the purplish shadows of <i>myth-in-itself</i>. Severian's story is a narrative dream hatched from the timbered depths of moral perturbability and painted in the hues of enigmatic unbinding. The aura of lush decay that permeates the first book provides an ambiance Wolfe can never <i>quite</i> attain to, let alone transcend, in the following three pieces; but the route each of the three provides for the journey towards self-realization for the Torturer Apprentice are, nonetheless, worthy companions to the opening act.<br /><br />This is neither a Tolkien clone nor a genre-checklist fantasy-lite: Wolfe is a gifted writer who excels at painting sombre moods and shifting scenery, at casting mysterious strangers to beset the torturer and scattering hints that Severian's story is not <i>all</i> what it seems; and he expects the reader to make the effort to puzzle through his mysteries and render his veils. A thoroughly enjoyable and sublime series that only gets better with further readings.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9122816075659472662" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2639877744" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2639877744" />
    <a title="David M" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43332343-david-m"><img alt="David M" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570428545p2/43332343.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2639877744?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David M" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David M" href="/user/show/43332343-david-m">David M</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2639877744" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3029773415808591566"><blockquote>Every long story, if it be told truly, will be found to contain all the elements that have contributed to the human drama since the first rude ship reached the strand of Lune...</blockquote><br/><br/>The world consists of one sedimented layer of apocalypse after another. In this situation it is almost as hard to believe in one last definitive end to everything as it is to believe in salvation. <br/><br/>Book of the New Sun is one of the great Catholic novels, but its vision of faith is in fact much more subtle than Brideshead R</span>
  <span id="freeText3029773415808591566" style="display:none"><blockquote>Every long story, if it be told truly, will be found to contain all the elements that have contributed to the human drama since the first rude ship reached the strand of Lune...</blockquote><br /><br />The world consists of one sedimented layer of apocalypse after another. In this situation it is almost as hard to believe in one last definitive end to everything as it is to believe in salvation. <br /><br />Book of the New Sun is one of the great Catholic novels, but its vision of faith is in fact much more subtle than Brideshead Revisited or the End of the Affair. <br /><br />Epic and endlessly baroque as it is, in the end it cannot but be a gesture towards some final miracle. An orientation. Apophantic science fiction?<br /><br />While I'm by no means an expert in the canon, I have to think this is the greatest work of science fiction ever published. Low pulp and high modernism; regardless of how one categorizes it, surely one of the greatest novels of the twentieth century. <br /><br />***<br /><blockquote>No one hesitates to brand as mad the solipsists who truly believe that the world exists only because they observe it and that buildings, mountains, and even ourselves (to whom they have spoken only a moment before) all vanish when they turn their heads. Is it not equally mad to believe that the meaning of the same objects vanishes in the same way? - pp 162 </blockquote><br /><br />Not sure of the answer. I think that's the right question. Severian, c'est moi.<br /><br />He was apparently inspired by a guy in a costume at a sci fi convention. I still think that's corny as hell. Oh well. <br /><br /><br />**********<br />{my reactions on completing my first reading, on the last day of the year 2018}<br /><br />To the good people of Goodreads,<br /><br />Happy new year and thank you for your friendship over the years. At the risk of immodesty, I'll say that before joining this website I'd never met anyone who'd read as many obscure and difficult art novels as I had in my life. I'm happy to say this is no longer the case, and wish to thank you all for making me a little less alone. <br /><br />I would ask you now to consider reading this very strange book - or sequence of books - on which my critical faculties have stumbled. The unimaginative have called it the <i>Ulysses</i> of science fiction, but all they seem to mean by that is that it's not exactly an easy read. I would opine that Gene Wolfe is really closer to Proust with his horrible longueurs of memory and desire. Proust plus monsters, set in a universe arguably not governed by the standard laws of physics. <br /><br /><i>Arguably</i> because in many ways I feel like I failed this opus on my first reading. I'm really not sure what the hell just happened. It exudes a certain atmosphere. The premise would continue to haunt me even at times when the actual act of reading it became arduous. See what you can make of it, friends.<br /><br />as ever,<br />d</span>
  <a data-text-id="3029773415808591566" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_149732922" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/149732922" />
    <a title="Adrienne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/384315-adrienne"><img alt="Adrienne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1351915628p2/384315.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/149732922?book_show_action=true">Feb 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adrienne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adrienne" href="/user/show/384315-adrienne">Adrienne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/384315-adrienne?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/384315-adrienne?shelf=unreliable-narrator">unreliable-narrator</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/384315-adrienne?shelf=assassins-thieves-etc">assassins-thieves-etc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/384315-adrienne?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/384315-adrienne?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/384315-adrienne?shelf=first-person">first-person</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/384315-adrienne?shelf=dense">dense</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/384315-adrienne?shelf=post-book-withdrawl">post-book-withdrawl</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer149732922" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16186559024302601809">When I finished this book, I wrote:<br/><blockquote>...I think I've got to think about this book for a little bit. I was liking it well enough (Severian was even growing on me!) and then...<br/><br/>Now I'm moving on to something <b>totally different</b>.</blockquote><br/>But really I was just lying to myself (just like Severian does). I proceeded to reread the first 100 pages of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40992.Shadow___Claw__The_Book_of_the_New_Sun___1_2_' title='Shadow & Claw (The Book of the New Sun, #1-2) by Gene Wolfe' rel='nofollow noopener'>Shadow and Claw</a> and then spent two hours googling various interpretations. Finally, after a good long while of thinking and brow furrowing, I started reading something e</span>
  <span id="freeText16186559024302601809" style="display:none">When I finished this book, I wrote:<br /><blockquote>...I think I've got to think about this book for a little bit. I was liking it well enough (Severian was even growing on me!) and then...<br /><br />Now I'm moving on to something <b>totally different</b>.</blockquote><br />But really I was just lying to myself (just like Severian does). I proceeded to reread the first 100 pages of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40992.Shadow___Claw__The_Book_of_the_New_Sun___1_2_" title="Shadow &amp; Claw (The Book of the New Sun, #1-2) by Gene Wolfe" rel="nofollow noopener">Shadow and Claw</a> and then spent two hours googling various interpretations. Finally, after a good long while of thinking and brow furrowing, I started reading something else. But unlike Severian, I actually told you what I did instead of talking my way around it. If he were writing this, he'd probably mention that he moved on and then many pages later drop some hint about what he figured out on page whatever of Shadow and Claw.<br /><br />So: if you like puzzles, well then. This series may be for you. Unfortunately I feel like it's manipulating my puzzle-loving mind into <i>thinking about it all the time</i>. That would have been okay if I had really enjoyed the characters or the plot in the books.<br /><br />But instead I feel like I'm being dragged kicking (but not screaming) back into the story simply because my mind wants to figure out it. I'm not eager to read them again for the characters or for the plot, but I want to because <i>I don't understand them yet</i>. And I think that's the source of my ambivalence.<br /><br />I will admit that Severian has grown on me since the first book, mostly because he's finally started to grow up by the beginning of this book. He only continues to grow (in oh-so-many ways) throughout the course of the books. But it's only a wary sort of acceptance of him on my part; I don't think I'd like to spend any time with him, but I no longer want to roll my eyes and say "Oh come on, kid. Really?!" Or punch him, for that matter. He's still notoriously unreliable. We also come to understand why he has to be so... detached from the narrative. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The royal we has never been so fitting. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> So that's good.<br /><br />That's not to say that there weren't a few "come on, really?!" moments, but they were directed more towards the plot than the main character. And some "oh no!!" moments. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I understand why Terminus Est had to go, but I didn't realize how attached I'd gotten to that sword. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. And some "oh whoa, that's what this world is like!" realizations. (Those were awesome, and so well done because the same thing had been mentioned, in passing, many pages before I picked up on it.) But then there were the few squicky moments near the end. While off-putting events happen throughout the series, the last <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[about Dorcas, at the inn <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> has certainly colored many things for me on any potential reread. (I'm also morbidly curious as to what the excruciation called <i>the two apricots</i> is, and I have some guesses, but it's probably worse for us to imagine what it means.)<br /><br />Happily, I am less grumbly about some of the science relating to the sun in this world. Unfortunately, something else about it has made me grumbly. But I think I'll have to read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/60215.The_Urth_of_the_New_Sun__The_Book_of_the_New_Sun___5_" title="The Urth of the New Sun (The Book of the New Sun, #5) by Gene Wolfe" rel="nofollow noopener">The Urth of the New Sun</a> to figure out how I actually feel about it. And I will read it, as soon as it arrives at the library (unless its place is usurped by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1215032.The_Wise_Man_s_Fear__Kingkiller_Chronicle___2_" title="The Wise Man's Fear (Kingkiller Chronicle, #2) by Patrick Rothfuss" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wise Man's Fear</a>...).<br /><br />Anyway, thanks to my officemate and friend for letting me ramble on about it for probably at least an hour. We did compare similarities between this series and her book ("Wait, your book has a crazy cult too?!", "I'm not sure if the narrator is telling the truth! Sometimes we disagree on what's true!", etc.), so that was fun.<br /><br />So: I'm pretty sure I'll re-read this series. I just wish I liked the characters and the story enough to feel like solving the puzzles is worthwhile for reasons other than the satisfaction you get from having solved them.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16186559024302601809" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2349224" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2349224" />
    <a title="John Wiswell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/147818-john-wiswell"><img alt="John Wiswell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442531848p2/147818.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2349224?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Wiswell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Wiswell" href="/user/show/147818-john-wiswell">John Wiswell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fantasy readers, sci fi readers, literary fiction readers, fans of world building</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2349224" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2706231645627905935">This volume picks up and concludes Gene Wolfe's Book of the New Sun quartet. Most readers will be absolutely lost without reading the first two volumes.<br/><br/>Re-entering Wolfe's vision of the future, the tone is the same. Severian is as preachy and detached as ever, though readers will be relieved to understand how he got this way, as revealed towards the end of the second book. This does not excuse the needlessly somber and coldly overanalytical style of narration for the rest of these books, though,</span>
  <span id="freeText2706231645627905935" style="display:none">This volume picks up and concludes Gene Wolfe's Book of the New Sun quartet. Most readers will be absolutely lost without reading the first two volumes.<br /><br />Re-entering Wolfe's vision of the future, the tone is the same. Severian is as preachy and detached as ever, though readers will be relieved to understand how he got this way, as revealed towards the end of the second book. This does not excuse the needlessly somber and coldly overanalytical style of narration for the rest of these books, though, where it only serves to hinder the fluidity of storytelling. Wolfe's narrator drops major events, like an important character's death, on the audience artlessly and moves on with unsatisfying speed. Beyond a couple of paragraphs, many huge plot events seem to have no ramifications throughout <i>Sword of the Lictor,</i> and for a healthy part of <i>Citadel of the Autarch</i>. Progression is often clumsy and when Severian contemplates the story, he views it with a cold philosophy and analysis that does not remotely resemble how people would actually react, and that is so detatch and clinical that it isn't entertaining, either - that he becomes something other than a normal person does not excuse the often painful prose across the seven hundred pages before the change. The collected first person perspective of the story also hides or bluntly announces any character development, turning the storyteller into a barrier between the readers and the fiction. Because these latter two books have more adventure than first two, the style of storytelling is even more irksome, though Wolfe occasionally nails an action scene, such as a monster's attack on a small house in <i>Sword of the Lictor.</i> Readers who have made it this far will likely enjoy the sprawling strangeness of Urth, from strange creatures to a growing sense of its culture in things like a storytelling contest Severian recounts to us at the beginning of the final book.<br /><br /><i>Sword</i> sees the best relationship in the series, between Severian and Young Severian, a child whom our protagonist takes under his wing. The relationship doesn't thrive on fakely sentimentality or cliche, but opens up the mind of our narrator and produces more real human emotion than the revenge-based, economic or sexual-political relationships he explores with so many adult characters. The book also has the best ending, in that this one isn't abrupt isn't nearly as random as the close of the first two installments.<br /><br /><i>Citadel</i> returns to the standard of an anticlimactic ending. Considering how little plot exists in the over eight hundred pages of Wolfe's story, one can't really expect satisfying climaxes or conclusions, and the end to the final book reminds you of it, not with a battle or even a series of interesting actions, but in exposition and conversations. Conversation is the strength of the series, getting to more profound or interesting topics, or at least getting at them better than Severian does when he's on his own as narrator, but that doesn't make it a good ending. The major revelation at the conclusion is something most of the books haven't even considered, so it falls flat. This is made more annoying by Wolfe's heavy emphasis on theology, philosophy and governmental structures that we seldom see in action, but constantly hear about. Indeed, the weakest aspect of the whole quartet is that between the detached first person narrator and the frequent exposition, you read about a story more than you read a story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2706231645627905935" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_57983162" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/57983162" />
    <a title="Henrik" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/573337-henrik"><img alt="Henrik" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442495303p2/573337.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/57983162?book_show_action=true">May 31, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Henrik" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Henrik" href="/user/show/573337-henrik">Henrik</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Henrik by: </span>
      Christina Stind Rosendahl
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/573337-henrik?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/573337-henrik?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer57983162&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer57983162" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8791061250425854171"><b>The Sword of the Lictor:</b><br/><br/>I still greatly enjoy the tale and the carefully constructed sentences, but in this, the Third Book, I found myself wading in places, wondering what the idea was with this-or-that scene. The kind of wondering, mind you, that isn't good. I wasn't exactly bored (you can guess that from the rating) but at times I was less entertained and intrigued than other times.<br/><br/>While some characters actually show up again and we learn what the <i>really</i> are up to (this was great--the whole D</span>
  <span id="freeText8791061250425854171" style="display:none"><b>The Sword of the Lictor:</b><br /><br />I still greatly enjoy the tale and the carefully constructed sentences, but in this, the Third Book, I found myself wading in places, wondering what the idea was with this-or-that scene. The kind of wondering, mind you, that isn't good. I wasn't exactly bored (you can guess that from the rating) but at times I was less entertained and intrigued than other times.<br /><br />While some characters actually show up again and we learn what the <i>really</i> are up to (this was great--the whole Dr. Talos &amp; Baldaners element was very fascinating and set up, it turned out, quite an exciting climax of the Book) a lot of other characters and scenes appear to be random and without any real purpose, which annoys me. Of course, this can still change in Book Four;-)<br /><br />I liked how we follow our protagonist's emotional and moral development, but unfortunately they remain somewhat distanced for my taste. I think this is due to the fact that everything is told by the protagonist in hindsight, leaving a sort of blanket between him and everything--also these, very important, facts. So I find it hard to really care about, in the end. That is... I do... But not as much as I think the actual scenes warrant. For instance, the boy he is walking with for a little while quite unexpectedly dies (and violently so!); a shocking enough event, but our torturer's descriptions of his emotional tormoil and agony didn't ring "true" for me; it was too detached.<br /><br />I do like how the tale is a sort of adventure tale on par with the classics, such as <i>The Odyssey</i>, with all the same ramifications of learning, growing up etc., etc. It is also good to see how the huge sword he has carried with him turns out to be more influental than expected, and to learn more about the mysterious "trinket" he carries with him (the Claw); the latter in particular looks like it's very important for the whole plot. But it's too bad too much seems weirdly disjointed. For my taste, at any rate.<br /><br />It is all still a masterpiece, but I do hope the Fourth Book turns out better than this one.<br /><br /><b>The Citadel of the Autarch:</b><br /><br />Excellent--in a very demanding way. Just like the whole Series, really. Fortunately Book 4 manages to rise the level of things, so now I am forced to give 5 stars;-)<br /><br />What's interesting is that when I closed the book I wasn't really much the wiser on the world of Urth and what was going on!? Yet I cannot but admire this book. Okay, there are a few war scenes in this book that I did not enjoy that much (I generally tend to be a little bored whenever I encounter such scenes in stories), but they did make sense and they did help the protagonist evolve and learn things. And considering that we have heard about this war throughout the story it was only right that Severian got to be there in person. So they needed to be there. Just like everything else in the story, which in my opinion meet ends excellently, if not always conclusively, in this book.<br /><br />My favourite part is without exception The Last House sequence. For me it had reminiscences of Hodgson's <i>The House on the Borderlands</i>, with its dizzying concepts of travelling through the universe and witnessing the death of planets. Here we are not travelling the universe but we are seeing various stages of Urth dying at different times. It was mindblowing and so well-conceived that I was breathtaken, which happens very rare nowadays when I read a book. So it says something. That the various stages were also quite realistic (in real, non-fiction life terms) only added to this experience--not to mention adding a feeling of dread.<br /><br />Really, you have to read it for yourself to see what I mean;-)<br /><br />I had a laugh (literally) when a <i>deus ex machina</i> prop popped up... that is <i>came down from the sky</i>... and actually introduced itself by defending an author's right to use such a device to move a story forward! How often does that happen, eh? And the weird thing is--in the end it made sense in this story. After all, the underlying suggestion of the protagonist <i>destined</i> to do the things he do are revealed to really <i>be</i> part of an almost mechanical world system (but with static time as the "playground" of that world system, so it's rather different from e.g. a Newtonian mechanism), and really <i>be</i> a prophecy come true--with the help of a lot of shadowy, vision-like, and genereally always unknown powerful players in the background making sure Severian ends up being the ruler, the Autarch.<br /><br />The "quest" element is neatly solved, and explained where it needs explanation, so we do get a "satisfying end," but lots of lots of things are left unexplained. In fact, the book ends with our dear protagonist getting ready to leave Urth for the stars--without knowing whether he will succeed in ultimate success or not. All of which seems to be tied up with a larger scheme of things, the history of Urth and the destiny of mankind. Perhaps past, present <i>and</i> future... As such can be said to exist in a static time scenario. Not to mention that it is tied up with science, religion, philosophy, storytelling (btw--there are lots of almost fairytale-like stories in this book, told by people the protagonist meet)... etc., etc. ...<br /><br />There's so much in here. Even if I spent the rest of my life reading these books I am not sure I would grasp everything in them.<br /><br />I kinda like that;-)</span>
  <a data-text-id="8791061250425854171" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1885410316" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1885410316" />
    <a title="Xantha Page" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/64053858-xantha-page"><img alt="Xantha Page" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1498706322p2/64053858.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1885410316?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Xantha Page" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Xantha Page" href="/user/show/64053858-xantha-page">Xantha Page</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/64053858-xantha-page?shelf=novels-and-novellas">novels-and-novellas</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/64053858-xantha-page?shelf=science-fantasy">science-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/64053858-xantha-page?shelf=scifi-etc">scifi-etc</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1885410316" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7212293444680324075">Reads like the sort of book Borges would've written about in one of his stories. It is replete with Borgesian and Borges-adjacent symbols: mirrors and labyrinths, masks, masques, and embedded narratives of all kinds. Its first-person narrator is not too far off from that of the unwritten novel Borges and Bioy Casares discuss in the second paragraph of "Tlön, Uqbar, Orbis Tertius," (who deceives the reader) or that of the main part of "The Immortal" (who draws from more than one person's memory).</span>
  <span id="freeText7212293444680324075" style="display:none">Reads like the sort of book Borges would've written about in one of his stories. It is replete with Borgesian and Borges-adjacent symbols: mirrors and labyrinths, masks, masques, and embedded narratives of all kinds. Its first-person narrator is not too far off from that of the unwritten novel Borges and Bioy Casares discuss in the second paragraph of "Tlön, Uqbar, Orbis Tertius," (who deceives the reader) or that of the main part of "The Immortal" (who draws from more than one person's memory). <i>The Book of the New Sun</i> is rather like the encyclopedia <i>Orbis Tertius</i>. It's a science fiction novel disguised as a heroic fantasy in the form of a memoir, and stands in relation to those genres as a synthesis and attempt as apotheosis.<br /><br />The narrator is perhaps the novel's greatest success. He has the rare quality (for the narrator of a secondary-world fiction) of writing as if he were part of the world that he describes. Certain details, such as the color of the moon or the daytime sky, which in any other science fiction novel, since they are different from our own, would be established in the first chapter if not on the first page are not alluded to until he finds it appropriate to do so, naturally, in the course of his narrative. He describes things, to the best of his ability, that he does not fully understand. In these and other ways much of the "backstory" that is normally forgrounded in works of speculative fiction is communicated in the margins, by implication and suggestion.<br /><br />The narrator himself is equally elusive and elliptical. His narrative has a clear chronological structure, yet he often narrates things out of order, going on tangents both philosophical and biographical, or remembering the process of remembering. He claims to have a perfect memory, yet he mixes up details on several occasions, or witholds information for sometimes guessable, somethings seemingly inexplicable reasons. He even admits to lying to people who figure in his narrative. It is often said that Severian is an "unreliable narrator," and this is true but perhaps misleading, or better yet it is only a synechdoche for the text as a whole. It is a window into another world, yet an imperfect one, just like any text "about" the real world.<br /><br />The novel has a beautifully elliptical structure. At first it seems episodic and arbitrary, but the more you read the more a dazzling and instricate pattern falls into place.<br /><br />Reading about the book, I've often seen its biggest fans treat it as a sort of literary puzzle box, a book that "demands greater attention" than the typical work of science fiction, challenging the reader to unlock its innermost secrets and parse out all the details of Wolfe's fictional history. This is also the tack of some of the novel's critics, who frame the same thing as a clever but pointless exercise, the product of an author's totalitarian pen. I suppose this would fit in with Wolfe's Catholicism (a strong running theme, hammered home especially at the novel's end); after all, the narrator, in one of the novel's frequent metafictional asides, prodivdes a three-part typology of allegory not dissimilar to the four-part medieval Christian one. To dispel this reductionism one must simply let the text speak for itself. Severian wanders the maze of his own life, and it is not one that anyone ever fully maps out, nor could they. We can wander with him for a time, and then we must go our own way.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7212293444680324075" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1870586537" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1870586537" />
    <a title="Crito" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35959535-crito"><img alt="Crito" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1413342130p2/35959535.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1870586537?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Crito" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Crito" href="/user/show/35959535-crito">Crito</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35959535-crito?shelf=sff-genre">sff-genre</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1870586537" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16624218141545059234">Gene Wolfe has a perhaps earned reputation for literary genre fiction. Immediately that puts expectations in certain places; that he's either impossibly dense, obscure, and convoluted, or he's this writer with rich, luscious prose teeming with symbolism and philosophical musings. The first is flat wrong, and the second is misleading for the same reason as the first. Though definitely stylized, Wolfe's writing is fairly straightforward. The narrative has two feet planted squarely in fantasy and i</span>
  <span id="freeText16624218141545059234" style="display:none">Gene Wolfe has a perhaps earned reputation for literary genre fiction. Immediately that puts expectations in certain places; that he's either impossibly dense, obscure, and convoluted, or he's this writer with rich, luscious prose teeming with symbolism and philosophical musings. The first is flat wrong, and the second is misleading for the same reason as the first. Though definitely stylized, Wolfe's writing is fairly straightforward. The narrative has two feet planted squarely in fantasy and is recognizable by design. What's clever about it is the original conceit of using archaic English and classical words sprinkled in here and there to replace your typical slipshod fantasy-isms, which gives the writing that essential "old and forgotten yet familiar" feeling, and yet it's clean and refined as opposed to Tolkien's "let's make this sound like a 19th century prose translation of The Odyssey" style, which felt more clumsy than quaint. Wolfe then builds his story with the same ethos of his prose by running his protagonist through so many typical genre conventions, and then veering sharply away from them as soon as you start building expectations. <br />It bears pointing out that Wolfe doesn't have a bone to pick with genre fiction, and he certainly isn't satirizing it or mocking it by twisting its tropes. This is a genre novel you can read like a genre novel. He's just making use of the tools and bending them to fit what he wants to do. It's almost a breath of fresh air to read genre fic that isn't set on explaining everything about the world, taking every two steps to explain how the plumbing works. He's not deliberately obfuscating anything by doing this, he's glossing over details when they serve no actual purpose. If anything, this serves as streamlining so the real meat of the story will stand out. The more truly literary aspects step out of the shadows later on when you've been on the train too long to hop off. The highlight of this is in the last book when it briefly turns into a Decameron-like frame narrative for different characters to tell stories. The stories one tells inherently tells a lot about their personality and the culture they come from; it follows that this section proves better lore and worldbuilding than any inane description could offer. The highlight has to be the story told through interpretations of a character's Confucian/scripture-like aphorisms.<br />So it's clear that stories and the way people tell them fascinate Wolfe, he peppers these stories all throughout The Book of the New Sun. This ties into memory and interpretation, of which the former is probably the most important theme. The theme of memory ties into the motif of cycles and the prose itself, taking old forgotten words and recontextualizing them into something new, and in the narrative by repeating played out tropes and then twisting them into something different and foreign. By far though the most interesting thing for me was the repeating and multiplying motif of the connections people build between each other, the symbiotic relations people form in various ways. Early on our young hero Severian, having been a shut in adolescent, shockingly takes to wanting to fuck every woman he sees. While this is formula for sexual wish fulfillment in any other genre novel, it's made a topic of subversion in the first book, and by the last book it's turned into a thing of abstract spiritual connection.<br />Needless to say though, deployment of a cliche inevitably admits to it. Severian does in fact wander around on a quest carrying a meme sword and a mcguffin gem, fighting monsters, getting in the middle of warring factions, and so on. It's genre. The greatest result of the grueling work put into this thing was simply, for myself, the ability enjoy a genre work at face value without the usual compromises you have to put up with. It's a late bloomer on enjoying it as literature. And I should mention that a ton of the devices Wolfe uses, from the small details like the famed astronaut picture, the fact of Severian's narration and assumed unreliability, the tidbits of philosophy here and there, and implications behind his word usage and who characters are named after: all this is window dressing. The Wolfe fans get absolutely froggy over the density of it but mostly it amounts to easter eggs that are absolutely not necessary to mull over in order to get what The Book of the New Sun is doing. It isn't the minor details where Wolfe lives up to his reputation, it's in the overall project of it. If anything that means he comes out more unique than he already proved himself to be. Strong recommendation. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16624218141545059234" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_205948729" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/205948729" />
    <a title="Mark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/316799-mark"><img alt="Mark" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1196198918p2/316799.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/205948729?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark" href="/user/show/316799-mark">Mark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">EVERYONE</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/316799-mark?shelf=sciencefiction">sciencefiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer205948729" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9819426866462016775">Sheesh! I originally wrote a review for Gene Wolfe's "Sword and Citadel". It was brilliant, pulitzer-prize-winning prose. However, the computer I was using while writing it threw a blue screen of death at me right after I finished it. A familiar tale, I'm sure. It's the modern version of "the dog ate my homework". Sometimes, though, the dog really does eat your homework. What do you say then? Tell the truth or make up a more plausible lie?<br/><br/><b>
  <u>The Plot</u>
</b><br/><br/>Severian is a member of the torturer's guild.</span>
  <span id="freeText9819426866462016775" style="display:none">Sheesh! I originally wrote a review for Gene Wolfe's "Sword and Citadel". It was brilliant, pulitzer-prize-winning prose. However, the computer I was using while writing it threw a blue screen of death at me right after I finished it. A familiar tale, I'm sure. It's the modern version of "the dog ate my homework". Sometimes, though, the dog really does eat your homework. What do you say then? Tell the truth or make up a more plausible lie?<br /><br /><b>
  <u>The Plot</u>
</b><br /><br />Severian is a member of the torturer's guild. He's narrating the "Book of the New Sun" from his memory, which is eidectic. The series tells how he is thrown out of the torturer's guild, travels the world, and then ends up being the autarch of the commonwealth. The action of the book takes place in the far flung future, where the sun is dying and the earth has been depleted of its resources. Ancient technology capable of spanning the stars is sitting around like so much junk, and strange creatures, presumably imported to earth from the stars, run amok in the wilderness. The commonwealth, of which Severian is a citizen, is at war with the Ascians, a totalitarian society reminiscent of the one described in George Orwell's 1984. <br /> <br /><b>
  <u>The Good</u>
</b><br /><br />It seems that every word, every letter written in the pages of the "Book of the New Sun" was deliberately put there to add layers of meaning and symbolism and self-reference to the story as well as to advance the plot, of which there is plenty. <br /><br />Most fantasy authors seem to use neologisms, or made-up words, to fabricate their worlds. Wolfe eschews "made up" words in favor of archaic or obscure words. For example, he uses the word "claviger" to describe a prison guard. If you look up "claviger" in a dictionary, you'll see that it is "one who carries the keys of any place" or "one who carries a club; a club bearer". Either would be applicable to a prison guard.<br /><br />The names of people have meaning also, The names seem to be unique, made up names. However, if you do a search on the character's names, you'll see that these characters are named after historical or mythological people...and that the historical or mythological people these characters are named after have some similarity to their namesakes. For example, Dorcas is a female who, in Acts 9:39, is raised from the dead by the Apostle Peter. Dorcas, in the Book of the New Sun, is a woman who is raised from the dead by Severian.<br /><br />The plot also does funny things with the concept of time. When it's all said and done, the plot seems to fold in upon itself in strange, interesting, and wonderful ways.<br /><br />The Author also uses stories to flesh out the plot. You can tell a lot about a society by its stories, and this future society is no exception. There's a play within the story that seems to relate the biblical story of creation (with some changes). There's also a book that Severian keeps reading that contains stories that seem to have some kind of mystifying morality to them and perhaps are re-imaginings of ancient Greek, Roman, and Norse myths. Finally, there's a contest where a woman professes that she'll marry the man who tells the best story, and it includes a story by an Ascian, who has an odd but wonderful way of talking, so that she has to translate the story for the rest of the group. In my opinion, the story within a story is wonderful when done correctly, and it is definitely done correctly here.<br /><br /><b>
  <u>The Not-So-Good</u>
</b><br /><br />Some of the plot consists of long, drawn out passages of Severian traveling, punctuated by action. I felt the travelogue could have used some trimming. However, I do think there's a reason for this seemingly excessive plot. Although, it's not apparent to me.<br /><br />Severian has a knack for encountering events and people that are odd, to say the least. He seems to shrug off these events as just things that happen. He doesn't really ask any of the questions we would want asked in that same situation. However, this could just be strong characterization on Wolfe's part. I mean, Severian is narrating the story, and what may seem strange to us is commonplace to him.<br /><br />This is a dense book. It's not an easy read. Don't expect to blow through this on your beach vacation and still be able to figure out what's going on. It lends itself to more than one reading and many people have told me that they have gotten a lot out of a second or even third read of the Book of the New Sun. I don't know if I can really commit myself that fully to this book...or to any book for that matter.<br /><br /><b>
  <u>Conclusion</u>
</b><br /><br />I plan on reading the "coda" to the New Sun series; Urth of the New Sun. However, I think I need some brain candy after this series. It was not an easy read but it was a good read.<br /><br />The Book of the New Sun is a novel that transcends genre fiction and rips through the barriers into the arena of pure literary genius. This is literature, not sci-fi. This is something that can only be read actively and can, and has been, studied by others. You could probably write your doctoral thesis on this book if you can convince your advisor that it's not just another sci-fi book. It's a good read. Go forth and read it!<br /><br /><b>
  <u>Incomplete List of Obscure/Archaic words found in Sword and Citadel:</u>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <u>autochthons</u>
</b> -- The indigenous people of a place.<br /><b>
  <u>Yesod</u>
</b> -- from kabbalah. The sephirah of Yesod translates spiritual concepts into actions that unite us with God.<br /><b>
  <u>anpiels</u>
</b> -- from kabbalah. Anpiel is one of the angels charged by rabbis with the government of birds, for every known species was put under the protection of one or more angels.<br /><b>
  <u>sannyasins</u>
</b> -- Hindu. a type of monk. In this phase of life, the person develops vairāgya, or a state of dispassion and detachment from material life. He renounces all worldly thoughts and desires, and spends the rest of his life in spiritual contemplation. <br /><b>
  <u>cenobites</u>
</b> -- priests. monks. <br /><b>
  <u>acaryas</u>
</b> -- A hindu religious teacher.<br /><b>
  <u>aquastors</u>
</b> -- Greek. A being or entity formed by the power of concentrated thought.<br /><b>
  <u>Ver-thandi (or verthandi)</u>
</b> -- The Present. One of the old norse norns; three women, past, present and future who decide the fate of all.<br /><b>
  <u>Araton</u>
</b> -- The olympian spirit of Saturn. In the novel, this term relates to the planet itself.<br /><b>
  <u>Bethor</u>
</b> -- The olympian spirit of Jupiter. In the novel, this term relates to the planet itself.<br /><b>
  <u>Phaley</u>
</b> -- The olympian spirit of the sun. In the novel, this term relates to the sun itself.<br /><b>
  <u>platybelodons</u>
</b> -- An elephant-like creature from pre-history; now extinct.<br /><b>
  <u>uintathers</u>
</b> -- A rhinocerous-like creature from pre-history; now extinct.<br /><b>
  <u>cerbotanas</u>
</b> -- A blowgun.<br /><b>
  <u>fulgurant</u>
</b> -- Flashing like lightning; dazzlingly bright.<br /><b>
  <u>delator</u>
</b> -- Latin/Roman term for someone who accuses someone else in a court of law.<br /><b>
  <u>atelier</u>
</b> -- French word for the workshop of an artist.<br /><b>
  <u>evzones</u>
</b> -- Greek term for light infantry who fought on mountainous terrain.<br /><b>
  <u>orpiment</u>
</b> -- A type of sulphur containing mineral. A favorite of alchemists, used in trade in the ancient Roman empire, and used medicinally by the Chinese.<br /><b>
  <u>Algedonic</u>
</b> -- characterized by or relating to pain especially as associated with pleasure.<br /><b>
  <u>argosy</u>
</b> -- An archaic term for a flotilla.<br /><b>
  <u>pelagic</u>
</b> -- Of, relating to, or living in open oceans or seas rather than waters adjacent to land or inland waters.<br /><b>
  <u>orphicleide</u>
</b> -- A keyed brass instrument of the bugle family with a baritone range that was the structural precursor of the bass saxophone and was replaced by the tuba in orchestras.<br /><b>
  <u>onyger</u>
</b> -- a donkey-like animal.<br /><b>
  <u>graisle</u>
</b> -- a type of horn used in battle.<br /><b>
  <u>peltasts</u>
</b> -- was a type of light infantry in Ancient Greece who often served as skirmishers.<br /><b>
  <u>contus</u>
</b> -- Roman word for a greek weapon that was a type of long wooden cavalry lance used by Iranian, especially Achaemenid successors' cavalry, most notably cataphracts. It was also used by the Germanic warriors of the south as a pike.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9819426866462016775" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
