<div id="review_1410127765" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1410127765" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9698647-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1341102729p2/9698647.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1410127765?book_show_action=true">May 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/9698647-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9698647-matt?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9698647-matt?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9698647-matt?shelf=2019-reads">2019-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1410127765" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14840416234941193223">The continuation of the classic story of an unremarkable young man who finds himself rising to the leadership of his nation. <i>Sword & Citadel</i> by Gene Wolfe is the omnibus collection of the last two volumes of <i>The Book of the New Sun</i> tetralogy, <i>The Sword of the Lictor</i> and <i>The Citadel of the Autarch</i>, following the travels of exiled torturer Severian.<br/><br/><i>The Sword of the Lictor</i> finds Severian arrived in Thrax performing his duties until he doesn’t kill someone for the city’s Archon and runs for the hill</span>
  <span id="freeText14840416234941193223" style="display:none">The continuation of the classic story of an unremarkable young man who finds himself rising to the leadership of his nation. <i>Sword &amp; Citadel</i> by Gene Wolfe is the omnibus collection of the last two volumes of <i>The Book of the New Sun</i> tetralogy, <i>The Sword of the Lictor</i> and <i>The Citadel of the Autarch</i>, following the travels of exiled torturer Severian.<br /><br /><i>The Sword of the Lictor</i> finds Severian arrived in Thrax performing his duties until he doesn’t kill someone for the city’s Archon and runs for the hills. He ultimately meets up and battles Dr. Talos and Baldanders in which his sword is destroyed. <i>The Citadel of the Autarch</i> finds Severian continuing his wanderings towards the war in the North when he stumbles upon it. Through his war experience he meets up with the Autarch and becomes his successor after eating him.<br /><br />The opening volume of the book, <i>Sword</i>, is the nadir of the entire series as I came to dislike Severian as a character and Wolfe as a writer because of awful everything was. What made it worse was that the concluding volume of the book, <i>Citadel</i>, began well and gave a false promise about redeeming the entire series then Severian meet up with the Autarch and it quickly went into the abyss. Wolfe wrote five “in-world” stories, one in <i>Sword</i> and four in <i>Citadel</i> as part of a storytelling contest, which were all better than either one of these volumes.<br /><br />I had high hopes for this classic series by Gene Wolfe, however <i>Sword &amp; Citadel</i> concluded one of the most overrated series I’ve ever read.<br /><br /><a href="https://mattries.wordpress.com/2019/05/06/the-sword-of-the-lictor/" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sword of the Lictor</a> (1.5/5)<br /><a href="https://mattries.wordpress.com/2019/05/12/the-citadel-of-the-autarch/" rel="nofollow noopener">The Citadel of the Autarch </a>(2/5)</span>
  <a data-text-id="14840416234941193223" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_10845840" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/10845840" />
    <a title="Agnieszka" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/704067-agnieszka"><img alt="Agnieszka" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1354899202p2/704067.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/10845840?book_show_action=true">Dec 21, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Agnieszka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Agnieszka" href="/user/show/704067-agnieszka">Agnieszka</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Catholic science fiction fans</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer10845840" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15258395302333539018">My three favorite novels in the world are Dune by Frank Herbert, The Name of the Rose by Umberto Eco, and The Book of the New Sun by Gene Wolfe. I bet that many of you have read, and many more have heard of, the first two, but I wonder how many have read the last. The Book of the New Sun is less accessible than The Name of the Rose and weirder than Dune. The mind-bending future world, where the sun is so close to dead that you can see the stars in the daytime, is on par with Dune in its richness</span>
  <span id="freeText15258395302333539018" style="display:none">My three favorite novels in the world are Dune by Frank Herbert, The Name of the Rose by Umberto Eco, and The Book of the New Sun by Gene Wolfe. I bet that many of you have read, and many more have heard of, the first two, but I wonder how many have read the last. The Book of the New Sun is less accessible than The Name of the Rose and weirder than Dune. The mind-bending future world, where the sun is so close to dead that you can see the stars in the daytime, is on par with Dune in its richness and complexity. The (mostly religious) philosophy, which the characters often discuss in casual conversation without it seeming contrived, is on par with The Name of the Rose or better. But the characterization is better than either. Although the main character is male, the female characters are far more real, vivid, and varied than almost anything I've encountered in fiction, and particularly science fiction.<br /><br />The book is full of hints and allusions and puzzles. Despite what some readers may say, all the pieces are there and it's quite possible to put them together, though it might take a second read-through and some thought. I think many readers come away frustrated because they read the first volume alone and find that they have more questions than answers. There's also Gene Wolfe's love of obscure vocabulary--he doesn't make up words, he just resurrects them. I liked learning that an epopt is the opposite of a neophyte and that a carnifex is another word for executioner. You can get by with context, but if you're a word nerd the OED will beckon you. Every now and again the narrative pauses while the characters read a story from a book of tales or have a story-telling context. The narrator-main character tries to fool you into thinking that it's just an interlude included for his sense of completeness, but like many of things he says, it's just a subtle lie or perhaps self-deception.<br /><br />It's one of those books that I wish fervently my friends would all read, because it's such a pleasure to discuss. I'm not sure I've done a good job of convincing anyone, but if you're a fan of difficult pleasures, this is a book for you.<br />	<br />	</span>
  <a data-text-id="15258395302333539018" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_50942485" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/50942485" />
    <a title="Jared Millet" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1032237-jared-millet"><img alt="Jared Millet" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1566869109p2/1032237.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/50942485?book_show_action=true">Mar 30, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jared Millet" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jared Millet" href="/user/show/1032237-jared-millet">Jared Millet</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1032237-jared-millet?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1032237-jared-millet?shelf=far-future">far-future</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1032237-jared-millet?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1032237-jared-millet?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1032237-jared-millet?shelf=dark-fantasy">dark-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1032237-jared-millet?shelf=finally-got-around-to">finally-got-around-to</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer50942485" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15915916228704294716">Have you ever loved a book entirely, but were reluctant to recommend it to anyone because you were sure they wouldn’t “get it?” That’s how I feel about Wolfe’s <i>Book of the New Sun</i>. It looks, smells, and tastes like an epic SFF adventure, it has all the right parts, the right characters, the right kinds of incidents making up the story, and yet when you put it all together it’s something so utterly <i>other</i> from what decades of genre and storytelling conventions have taught us to expect that when my</span>
  <span id="freeText15915916228704294716" style="display:none">Have you ever loved a book entirely, but were reluctant to recommend it to anyone because you were sure they wouldn’t “get it?” That’s how I feel about Wolfe’s <i>Book of the New Sun</i>. It looks, smells, and tastes like an epic SFF adventure, it has all the right parts, the right characters, the right kinds of incidents making up the story, and yet when you put it all together it’s something so utterly <i>other</i> from what decades of genre and storytelling conventions have taught us to expect that when my SF book group read the first volume a few months ago, most of the reactions were along the lines of “Uh, what the hell is this?”<br /><br />In <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/50942453" rel="nofollow noopener">my review of <i>Shadow &amp; Claw</i></a>, I called the New Sun cycle a “coming of age” story, but I no longer think that’s true, at least in the traditional sense of a young man setting out to discover himself. If anything, Severian the Torturer spends the entirety of the second half of the story <i>losing</i> himself, shedding his old identity piece by piece until he has no choice but to replace it with an entirely new(old) one. I commented on the fabulous sense of antiquity that Wolfe gives his dying Urth, but in Part 2 the focus flips so that Severian is drawn time and again to images, relics, and beings from the future(s). A central theme (though not the only one) in the New Sun is how the future influences us just as much as the past.<br /><br />It’s not giving anything away to reveal that Severian becomes the Autarch, ruler of Urth, by the end of the story, since it was heavily implied right from the beginning of vol.1. How he gets there, and what it actually means, are the true revelations. Definitely going to read this one again. Many times.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15915916228704294716" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris Gousopoulos" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris Gousopoulos" href="/user/show/7968109-chris-gousopoulos">Chris Gousopoulos</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781857987003" href="/book/show/274853.Sword_and_Citadel">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7968109-chris-gousopoulos?shelf=post-apocalypse-dystopia">post-apocalypse-dystopia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1248411872" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14486926680521193671">The whole series deserves a five rate if its viewed as a whole. A long Odyssey of richly imagined encounters of strange events and even stranger characters full of wondrous revelations. Many of these incidents might seem at first kind of random and maybe irrelevant but the concept is mulitilayered and connected with ties sometimes vague but strong enough when realised. What i liked most was the fact that while reading these books I spent much time of my day thinking of Severian's adventures and </span>
  <span id="freeText14486926680521193671" style="display:none">The whole series deserves a five rate if its viewed as a whole. A long Odyssey of richly imagined encounters of strange events and even stranger characters full of wondrous revelations. Many of these incidents might seem at first kind of random and maybe irrelevant but the concept is mulitilayered and connected with ties sometimes vague but strong enough when realised. What i liked most was the fact that while reading these books I spent much time of my day thinking of Severian's adventures and their implications on the universe, contemplating of lines spoken in well staged acts that hid philosophies and deeper meanings. Its not an action rich tale but those few intense moments are well written and they easily carry u into the heat of them. The writting in general is kinda weird and abstract and poetic, full of strange words most of them of Greek origin, something I enjoyed a lot since I am Greek, and found it an awesome way of giving a more obscure and serious meaning to certain concepts. All in all these books were a feast to imagination and thought provoking fiction. I did not manage to appreciate them when I was a teen and never got to finish the first book back then, but now the time was right and I am glad I took that second chance and was fully rewarded from it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14486926680521193671" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_812001407" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/812001407" />
    <a title="Stuart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart"><img alt="Stuart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452041473p2/9456990.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/812001407?book_show_action=true">Jan 04, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stuart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stuart" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart">Stuart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=dark-fantasy-gothic">dark-fantasy-gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=dying-earth">dying-earth</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer812001407" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17008783885131941021"><b> The Book of the New Sun: SFF’s greatest and most challenging epic</b><br/>Originally posted at <a href='http://www.fantasyliterature.com/reviews/the-shadow-of-the-torturer/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fantasy Literature</a><br/><b>THE BOOK OF THE NEW SUN</b> is considered by many SFF readers as the greatest, most challenging, and most rewarding SF-fantasy epic ever written in the genre. At the same time, its baroque language, ambiguous plot, unreliable narrator, and depth of symbolism are likely to discourage most casual readers. Therefore, new readers need to dedicate themselves to unraveling the many layers of plot, reli</span>
  <span id="freeText17008783885131941021" style="display:none"><b> The Book of the New Sun: SFF’s greatest and most challenging epic</b><br />Originally posted at <a href="http://www.fantasyliterature.com/reviews/the-shadow-of-the-torturer/" rel="nofollow noopener">Fantasy Literature</a><br /><b>THE BOOK OF THE NEW SUN</b> is considered by many SFF readers as the greatest, most challenging, and most rewarding SF-fantasy epic ever written in the genre. At the same time, its baroque language, ambiguous plot, unreliable narrator, and depth of symbolism are likely to discourage most casual readers. Therefore, new readers need to dedicate themselves to unraveling the many layers of plot, religious symbolism, literary references, and narrative sleight-of-hand. They also need to understand that it is essentially a single integrated work, so reading individual volumes is not enough to render judgement. You need to read all four volumes to appreciate what Gene Wolfe has painstakingly crafted. If you do so, you will be rewarded richly indeed.<br /><br />I’ve actually read the entire series twice in the past two decades, and decided that I would listen to the audiobook editions narrated by the excellent Jonathan Davis to give me a new perspective on the whole creation. He is the ideal narrator for a work this ambitious, as he tackles the baroque story with gravitas, confidence, and enthusiasm. I can’t imagine a narrator better suited to the task.<br /><b>THE BOOK OF THE NEW SUN</b> is that type of work, one that rewards multiple readings and reflection and still retains many of its mysteries tantalizingly out of reach. If you are someone comfortable with complexity, mysteries, and a lack of explanations, you should be able to enjoy Wolfe’s elusive style.<br /><br />This time I also prepared myself by reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25944503-between-light-and-shadow" rel="nofollow noopener">Marc Aramini’s Between Light and Shadow: An Exploration of the Fiction of Gene Wolfe, 1951 to 1986</a>, an 826-page analysis covering Wolfe’s output through 1986, including all of his short stories and his novels <b>The Fifth Head of Cerberus</b>, <b>Peace</b>, <b>Free Live Free</b>, and <b>THE BOOK OF THE NEW SUN</b>. Aramini’s analysis sheds much light on the key underlying themes of the story, namely the death and resurrection of the Urth via the coming of the New Sun, the ambiguous messianic nature of the protagonist Severian, the healing power of the Claw of the Conciliator, and Severian’s duty to undergo testing by alien powers to determine if humanity is indeed worthy of this rebirth.<br /><br />There are so many themes and ideas in Wolfe’s epic that it has spawned an informal school of analysis, including books such as <b>Michael Andre-Druissi’s Lexicon Urthus</b>, <b>Robert Borki’s Solar Labyrinth: Exploring Gene Wolfe’s “Book of the New Sun”</b> and <b>Peter Wright’s Attending Daedalus: Gene Wolfe, Artifice and the Reader</b>. Therefore, it would be presumptuous of me to try to analyze his magnum opus in a brief review such as this. Rather, I will just touch on the most important themes of the book and dispense with a discussion of plot details, which you can discover on your own.<br /><br />To drastically simplify things, <b>THE BOOK OF THE NEW SUN</b> is the story of the young apprentice Severian raised in the guild of torturers called the Seekers for Truth and Penitence. He recalls to the reader his complex path from lowly apprentice to the Autarch of the Commonwealth, the most powerful ruler on far-future Urth. This world lies millions of years in the future, to the point that our own world is no longer even a memory. It is a baroque fantasy world filled with mysterious terms such as archon, carnifex, cataphract, chalcedony, fuligin, hipparch, lazaret, monomachy, optimate, pelerine, psychopomp, quaesitor, thaumaturge, and uhlan. None of these terms are coined by Wolfe, but rather reflect his erudite love of obscure and archaic terms. They lend an air of incredible antiquity to the world of Urth, and the story takes direct inspiration from <b>Jack Vance's THE DYING EARTH</b>.<br /><br />Severian encounters all manner of friends &amp; foes, allies and enemies, lovers, thieves, soldiers, actors, priests, witches, commoners, malevolent creatures, inscrutable aliens, and other powerful beings whose intentions are unclear. What they all share is an interest in the fate of Severian, for though he does understand this himself for much of his journey, he is destined to not only become Autarch but to serve as the representative to the stars to determine if the dying red sun of Urth is worthy of being renewed in both a symbolic and literal sense, ushering in a New Sun and renewed era of humanity. It is an outcome that is constantly alluded to but never fully explained, though Wolfe later explored this is more detail (while still retaining much mystery) in a companion coda called <b>The Urth of the New Sun (1987)</b>.<br /><br />The religious symbolism of the story is both explicit and complicated. Severian is positioned as the obvious messiah, the living embodiment of the New Sun, and as he wields the cross-shaped executioners’ sword Terminus Est, the Christ-like imagery is plain for any reader to see. And yet throughout the story, Severian himself is a naive and conflicted character, one who struggles first to set aside his training as a torturer and executioner, and later as a man fighting to understand his role as a possible bringer of redemption and resurrection to a corrupt and dying world. This goes far beyond a Christian allegory such as <b>C.S. Lewis’ CHRONICLES OF NARNIA</b>, for instance. Of equal importance is the religious talisman that comes into Severian’s hands by accent, the Claw of the Conciliator. This object seems capable of bringing the dead back to life and healing fatal wounds when Severian wields it, and is associated with the Conciliator, a figure of the ancient past who is both Christ-like and may also have extra-terrestrial origins.<br /><br /><b>THE BOOK OF THE NEW SUN</b> also incorporates a number of stories-within-stories, told by various characters and also in the form of stage plays performed by the characters (echoing the story itself from the author’s perspective) during key moments in the narrative. One could write a dissertation on interpreting the meanings of these stories as they relate to the overall themes of the book, and this effort is far beyond me, but Marc Aramini does discuss the implications of a number these stories in his book <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25944503-between-light-and-shadow" rel="nofollow noopener">Marc Aramini’s Between Light and Shadow: An Exploration of the Fiction of Gene Wolfe, 1951 to 1986</a>, including Dr. Talos’ play “Eschatology and Genesis”, “The Tale of the Student and His Son”, “The Tale of the Boy Called Frog”, “The Cock, the Angel, and the Eagle”, “The Armiger’s Daughter”, as well as some extremely vivid dreams of Severian. This literary device is equally vital to understanding the layers of meaning in his book <b>Peace</b>.<br /><br />The story is further complicated by the roles of Abaia, Erebus, and their servants the undines, underwater beings of great power that seem intent on conquering humanity, but at the same time provide aid to Severian at times and may even benefit from the inundation of Urth if Severian is to succeed in bringing the New Sun. They are constantly observing Severians’ progress, but it is unclear whether they seek to aid or prevent his quest.<br /><br />We also have the even more inscrutable role of the Hierodules, alien beings from other worlds that reside on Urth and also take a keen interest in Severian and his path to becoming the Autarch. Despite their implied powers, they sometimes seem to indicate that they serve him, but this in never made entirely clear. They appear to have a connection with the current Autarch, but it is not apparent whether they favor Severian over his rule.<br /><br />Finally, there is the ambiguous role of the reigning Autarch himself, and his mysterious advisor Father Inire. They obviously wield great power, and if they were to decide Severian were a threat to them they could easily have him killed. Yet they instead appear in various guises in his adventures, never clearly his ally or enemy, always with opaque intentions and tantalizing comments. Father Inire in particular seems to control the power to travel between the stars via the sinister power of mirrors and labyrinths (an obvious nod to Jorge Luis Borges), but does not demonstrate to what end he might use them.<br /><br />In the end, <b>THE BOOK OF THE NEW SUN</b> does not easily yield up its secrets to interpretation or analysis, but this is most certainly the source of its lasting appeal to discerning literary SFF readers over the last 35 years. There are layers of meaning that remain obscure even after multiple readings, but it is clear that Wolfe has crafted a masterpiece that is worth the attempts to conquer it, a literary Mt. Everest that every serious reader in the genre will be drawn to again and again, and there is much to be gained in each attempt, whether the peak is attained or not.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17008783885131941021" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_795450086" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/795450086" />
    <a title="Bryan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16468510-bryan"><img alt="Bryan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1403848841p2/16468510.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/795450086?book_show_action=true">Dec 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bryan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bryan" href="/user/show/16468510-bryan">Bryan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer795450086" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11044792953328493596">A fantastic, genre-blending novel, and one which I am most pleased that I have read. If this is what Gene Wolfe's stuff is always like then I think I am going to have to hunt all of his writing down and devour it. If anything, parts 3 and 4 of The Book Of The New Sun series are even more thought-provoking than the first two, and that's saying something.<br/><br/>The Sword Of The Lictor: Having finally reached Thrax, Severian sure doesn't stay there very long. After a strange encounter involving the Claw, </span>
  <span id="freeText11044792953328493596" style="display:none">A fantastic, genre-blending novel, and one which I am most pleased that I have read. If this is what Gene Wolfe's stuff is always like then I think I am going to have to hunt all of his writing down and devour it. If anything, parts 3 and 4 of The Book Of The New Sun series are even more thought-provoking than the first two, and that's saying something.<br /><br />The Sword Of The Lictor: Having finally reached Thrax, Severian sure doesn't stay there very long. After a strange encounter involving the Claw, our "hero" narrowly escapes a weird salamander-type monster, splits with Dorcas after learning (some of) her true nature, rebels against his position as Lictor of Thrax by releasing a woman he is supposed to defenestrate and then flees into the wilderness. Actually, the majority of Sword comprises Severian's adventures out in the hinterlands, which makes for what is, in my opinion, the high point of the series. What I like about this book so much is that even though it's by far the most action-heavy part of the New Sun it still is thoughtful and laden with meaning. From Agia to the Alzabo, the strange beast-men to the sorcerers, there's definitely no shortage of action and adventure in Sword, and yet there are some truly touching scenes and themes - I'm thinking mainly of Severian's relationship with little Severian (even though he later shrugs off his death like a sociopath). Also, is little Severian actually another version of Severian himself!?! Hard to say. Regardless, Sword is possessed of some terrific scenes; namely, the confrontation with the megalomaniacal Typhon and also the final reveal and showdown with Baldanders (although in some ways it's tough to say exactly what was revealed at the end there).<br /><br />The Citadel Of The Autarch: By far the weirdest and most cerebral of the four New Sun novels, Citadel requires a lot of attention on the part of the reader, and even then you'll probably miss some stuff and have to go back and re-read certain passages in order for the whole to make any kind of sense (such was the case for me, at any rate). I'm not sure I should really bother with any sort of recap, as the events of this book involve a fair amount of confusing time-travel and also wouldn't really have any sort of meaning to someone unfamiliar with the series anyways. High points of Citadel: I really liked the complex language of the Ascians, and also the story-telling contest which Severian (sort of) judges in the camp of the Pelerines. Severian finally becomes the Autarch after consuming the flesh of the previous Autarch in another Alzabo ritual, gaining several hundred memories/personalities (which explains his detached nature as a narrator). The house-out-of-time, the return of the green man, the truth about Dorcas - all of these are pretty strange and only become stranger the more you think about them. All in all, I definitely wouldn't call Citadel a satisfying end to the series, as I was left wanting more, but I actually consider that a mark in its favour.<br /><br />I should note at this point that I haven't yet read The Urth Of The New Sun, the so-called coda to this excellent series. I'm looking forward to it, to say the least.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11044792953328493596" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_178241033" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/178241033" />
    <a title="Dergrossest" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1608480-dergrossest"><img alt="Dergrossest" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224169064p2/1608480.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/178241033?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dergrossest" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dergrossest" href="/user/show/1608480-dergrossest">Dergrossest</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer178241033" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1504007083560757132">These final volumes of The Book of the New Sun continue the travels of our Torturer-hero through the dying planet of Urth, our Earth, thousands of years in the future. Condemned by a rapidly fading Sun, hounded by bizarre beasts, surrounded by mysterious alien power brokers, warring against the mindlessly politically-correct remnants of North America, treating with flesh-eaters who absorb the consciousnesses of the dead, and dodging an ex-lover bent on his destruction with a legion of horrifical</span>
  <span id="freeText1504007083560757132" style="display:none">These final volumes of The Book of the New Sun continue the travels of our Torturer-hero through the dying planet of Urth, our Earth, thousands of years in the future. Condemned by a rapidly fading Sun, hounded by bizarre beasts, surrounded by mysterious alien power brokers, warring against the mindlessly politically-correct remnants of North America, treating with flesh-eaters who absorb the consciousnesses of the dead, and dodging an ex-lover bent on his destruction with a legion of horrifically monstrous assassins at her disposal, our hero loses the love of his life (who may be his mother) and possibly his younger self while ascending to the ultimate seat of power on the planet. Oh, and he just may be the Christ-like savior of humanity. And from the future, or the past or somewhere else altogether. Maybe the hundreds of other consciousnesses residing within him can help him figure it all out. Or not.<br /><br />Simultaneously philosophical, metaphysical and plain old heart-pounding adventure, this book has it all if you can deal with the somewhat unreliable narrator and the gaping holes gratuitously piercing the story throughout. It all worked brilliantly for me since the fantasy-meets-Sci-Fi writing is great, the characters lively and the setting unique. My only complaint is that there aren’t a dozen more books in the series.<br /><br />Highly recommended.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1504007083560757132" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_60098429" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/60098429" />
    <a title="Patrick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2141158-patrick"><img alt="Patrick" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/60098429?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick" href="/user/show/2141158-patrick">Patrick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer60098429" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12213791694136674703">This is the second half of Severian's bizarre journeys in Urth, which started with "Shadow and Claw." The latter was a very tough read, but I found Sword and Citadel to be easier, much in part because I got used to Wolfe's elaborate and shifting styles. It was still tough though, for the developments in this book really stretched conventional story telling. I would have to state that this book is masterful in its ideas and blending of the sci-fi and fantasy genres. I don't want to give anything </span>
  <span id="freeText12213791694136674703" style="display:none">This is the second half of Severian's bizarre journeys in Urth, which started with "Shadow and Claw." The latter was a very tough read, but I found Sword and Citadel to be easier, much in part because I got used to Wolfe's elaborate and shifting styles. It was still tough though, for the developments in this book really stretched conventional story telling. I would have to state that this book is masterful in its ideas and blending of the sci-fi and fantasy genres. I don't want to give anything away by describing the ingenious and thought provoking parts of the story, except to say they help in evolving the narration of Severian to a degree of bafflement at times. Also, there are parts in the book in which I lost faith in Severian, as his evidence for the seemingly supernatural elements of the Claw are confused week, and seemingly unreliable. I found this particular feeling to be an important element for the reader insofar as Severian's story is not merely an adventure against powerful antagonists, but also a journey of self discovery, an overall religious experience. This book is not for the weak: one needs stamina and a high ability to contextualize language as well as plot. I look forward to "The Urth of the New Sun," described as a coda to this series of books.Quote-right</span>
  <a data-text-id="12213791694136674703" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_60098429'><a id="like_count_review_60098429" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/60098429?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">2 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F40995.Sword_Citadel">Like</a></span>

<div id="review_2431337" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2431337" />
    <a title="Ross Lockhart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/145998-ross-lockhart"><img alt="Ross Lockhart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1391060033p2/145998.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2431337?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ross Lockhart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ross Lockhart" href="/user/show/145998-ross-lockhart">Ross Lockhart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2431337" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14435009835044411474">Completing Gene Wolfe’s dying earth tetralogy The Book of the New Sun, Sword and Citadel collects books three and four of the series, Sword of the Lictor and Citadel of the Autarch. As with Shadow and Claw, Wolfe’s language is sublime, but his storytelling, particularly when his convincing characters break form to share their own stories with one another, is easily among the best to be had. Here, Severian has begun to ply his trade, working as a lector (or torturer and executioner) in a city far</span>
  <span id="freeText14435009835044411474" style="display:none">Completing Gene Wolfe’s dying earth tetralogy The Book of the New Sun, Sword and Citadel collects books three and four of the series, Sword of the Lictor and Citadel of the Autarch. As with Shadow and Claw, Wolfe’s language is sublime, but his storytelling, particularly when his convincing characters break form to share their own stories with one another, is easily among the best to be had. Here, Severian has begun to ply his trade, working as a lector (or torturer and executioner) in a city far from the citadel and guild home where he began. Since that alone would make for a rather short epic, rest assured that by this volume’s end, Severian will encounter strange creatures, godlike beings, a stunning set piece best described as primitive villagers attacking Frankenstein’s castle, and, most shockingly, war. While Wolfe does continue to expand on this universe in later novels, including The Urth of the New Sun, which acts as coda to the tetralogy, The Book of the New Sun is a complete, self-contained, and satisfying work well worth reading.<br /></span>

    <a title="Nicole" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2442753-nicole"><img alt="Nicole" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1277854381p2/2442753.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/84645594?book_show_action=true">Jan 12, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicole" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicole" href="/user/show/2442753-nicole">Nicole</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2442753-nicole?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer17110649629569416472">Holy. Crap. I don’t think I’ve ever read a science fiction or fantasy novel that came so close to the complexity of other great works of fiction. It reminds me of Candide, Moby-Dick, many others. I don’t say that type of thing often – I promise I’m not being hyperbolic. This is super-literary bildungsroman stuff, that just happens to occur in the the far, far future on an Earth whose sun is going out.<br/><br/>After finishing this series of books, I immediately thought “Well, I’m going to have to reread t</span>
  <span id="freeText17110649629569416472" style="display:none">Holy. Crap. I don’t think I’ve ever read a science fiction or fantasy novel that came so close to the complexity of other great works of fiction. It reminds me of Candide, Moby-Dick, many others. I don’t say that type of thing often – I promise I’m not being hyperbolic. This is super-literary bildungsroman stuff, that just happens to occur in the the far, far future on an Earth whose sun is going out.<br /><br />After finishing this series of books, I immediately thought “Well, I’m going to have to reread these”. There’s no way I got it all the first time around. I really wish I had studied it in the context of a book group or class – I could’ve used some discussion, surely.<br /><br />Anyway, I’m pretty damn impressed, and will be reading some other stuff by Wolfe in the near future.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2802947559" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2802947559" />
    <a title="Patrick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8255719-patrick"><img alt="Patrick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1332087637p2/8255719.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2802947559?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick" href="/user/show/8255719-patrick">Patrick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781473212008" href="/book/show/33653478-sword-citadel">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2802947559" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer925562981351053688">It is sometimes said that the novels that make up Gene Wolfe’s 'Book of the New Sun' series work just as well as a bracing story of fantastic adventures as they do as anything else. I am not sure this is entirely true. It could be said of the first couple of instalments, perhaps, because they provide such a wealth of concrete detail that the later novels draw upon for the poetry of reminiscence. But by the time we get to 'The Sword of the Lictor' the author seems to be doing all he can to get aw</span>
  <span id="freeText925562981351053688" style="display:none">It is sometimes said that the novels that make up Gene Wolfe’s 'Book of the New Sun' series work just as well as a bracing story of fantastic adventures as they do as anything else. I am not sure this is entirely true. It could be said of the first couple of instalments, perhaps, because they provide such a wealth of concrete detail that the later novels draw upon for the poetry of reminiscence. But by the time we get to 'The Sword of the Lictor' the author seems to be doing all he can to get away from the expectations of a hero’s journey, and as we reach 'The Citadel of the Autarch' we find ourselves in a different sort of novel altogether. <br /><br />It is, at first, quite a literal departure. Severian finds himself once again leaving the place where he was stationed at the end of the last book. He even leaves his wife, Dorcas, who has grown strangely distant to him. Her condition seems analogous to a kind of depression or mania: not only has she realised the real nature of his vocation as a torturer, but her memories of her own death and resurrection have come back to haunt her. (Non-readers of Wolfe will have to bear with me; any concise summary of one of his storylines will necessarily sound impossibly bizarre.) So off he goes into the wilderness. <br /><br />Here the imagery becomes potent to the point of abstraction. The events are like dreams of events from the past two books, returning to us in a world that seems strangely emptied. Again we encounter Agia bent on revenge against Severian, but how notable that her reappearance is accompanied by one of Wolfe’s most notable creations: the Alzabo, a monster that seems to absorb the people it consumes in a way that goes beyond flesh and blood. In a very real sense their spirits become a part of this creature — their voices cry out from within it, like that dreadful bear in Alex Garland’s film of 'Annihilation' — but they also live a sort of afterlife in its corporeal form. It is a predatory, bestial version of the way in which Severian ate a piece of Thecla’s body in 'The Claw of the Conciliator' and took on her memories, her inner voice. <br /><br />In this and the next book the text becomes increasingly polyphonic. It is now not merely Severian’s place to wonder himself into the minds of others through speculation. He becomes them; or perhaps better to say, they become him. He meets a little boy who is also called Severian. Little Severian comes with him on his journey for a while. The two of them scale a mountain which is in fact carved into the likeness of a vast idol. Until, at one stage, the boy glimpses something like a giant ring of gold on the finger of the statue. He runs to touch it and is instantly vaporised. If any of this is of any metaphorical significance it is near impossible to tell — the book drifts on regardless. <br /><br />At the end of 'The Sword of the Lictor' is a set-piece of sorts. We are reunited with Dr Talos and Baldanders the giant from the previous novels; they are holed up in a tower, having convinced the locals of their innate superiority via technology that turns out to be on loan from visiting extraterrestrials. (I know. Stay with me.) The battle here might be the pinnacle of Severian’s adventuring. Yet by the end it only seems enormously wasteful. Motivation has vanished into the thicket of Wolfe’s gently deceptive prose. As we might have already suspected, it’s revealed that Talos and Baldanders had always held the inverse to their assumed roles. The doctor was a creation of the giant — as if the creature was the one who gave birth to Frankenstein. (Because after all, in a very real sense: didn’t he?) <br /><br />Severian’s sword is broken and his precious claw is lost. There’s something a little cheap in this realisation — perhaps the real claw was in him all along! Or perhaps there was no claw at all. But the healing, and the people he brought back to life — that was real, wasn’t it? Like many other great authors Wolfe can often be maddening. With him I almost always have the sense that surely he is making all this up as he goes along. Surely nobody would design a plot this outrageous. And yet from time to time we turn a corner in these books and view something like a giant pattern working desperately, almost invisibly, hidden somewhere just out of sight. <br /><br />***<br /><br />'The Citadel of the Autarch' is generally called the last book in this little quartet, even though the author would return to this universe for many novels yet. It is another departure. Severian is drafted into a sort of irregular army fighting a vast horde of enemies who are slowly descending from the north of his world. They are a people called the Ascians. It is a novel shaped throughout by the experience of war, both in its immediate violence and in its shape as a grand world-changing force. We only get to meet one of them, a prisoner, but it is in him that we are expected to see them all. The Ascians speak in a unique form of language which is entirely composed of tags and epigrams from Ascian texts. It is this sort of thing:<br /><br /><i>‘How shall the state be most vigorous? It shall be most vigorous when it is without conflict. How shall it be without conflict? When it is without disagreement. How shall disagreement be banished? By banishing the four causes of disagreement: lies, foolish talk, boastful talk, and talk which serves only to incite quarrels. How shall the four causes be banished? By speaking only Correct Thought. Then shall the state be without disagreement. Being without disagreement it shall be without conflict. Being without conflict it shall be vigorous, strong, and secure.’</i><br /><br />It’s hard not to feel that it must have been influenced by the author’s own experiences in the Korean war. This has, it seems, been a common interpretation, to the extent that some readers took ‘Ascian’ to effectively refer to ‘asian’. This is over-literal, and was rejected by Wolfe himself; but regardless, there’s something in the depiction that seems to resemble contemporary fears of Leninist groupthink. And yet words like these are not always what they seem. The Ascian prisoner finds a way of stringing together his tags so that they can be read allegorically — in this way, they become an entirely different story to the one he appears to be telling. There are so many stories in these books.<br /><br />I find it difficult to extract much that is explicitly political from 'The Book of the New Sun' thus far. The same is true of much of Wolfe’s work in general. Perhaps I am being naive. He is not great with women — that might be one starting point. As far as I can trace any inclination, it might be best defined as a dedication to the cult of the individual. Society at large (and especially government) seems an ugly, dangerous thing in these books. Better to put our faith in those who know best. Wolfe was, I know, Catholic, and that spirit is part of these novels. Certainly there is stuff here that could be considered obliquely spiritual. There are lines that could be said to be directly referencing transubstantiation — not least the idea of consuming the body and blood of another to become them. Close biblical readings are possible, and plentiful. But ‘to what extent can we consider Severian a Christ-like figure?’ seems to me an agonising question best left to high school essays. <br /><br />The Autarch of the title is the leader of this world. Better to call him the head of state, perhaps, since there seems to be nothing that compares to what we might call a single coherent structure of government. But he is above it all regardless. Near the end of this novel, Severian becomes the Autarch, or rather the Autarch becomes him. He swallows another potion which contains the essence of every Autarch who came before. Now he is no longer an individual in any meaningful sense (if he ever was): he is a legion of voices, thoughts, dreams from different times. He has effectively attained the unfailing, omnipresent memory that he always claimed to have. Yet he finds himself returning all the way back to the Citadel where we began this journey, back in The Shadow of the Torturer. His old dog is there. We return to the room full of clocks and sundials. Because what else is there, really, except for stories, and the love of a sad old dog?</span>
  <a data-text-id="925562981351053688" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1223138138" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1223138138" />
    <a title="Nye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33101792-nye"><img alt="Nye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1405481268p2/33101792.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1223138138?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nye" href="/user/show/33101792-nye">Nye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1223138138" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11584215065973985249">At the encouragement of a good friend of mine who has similar tastes, I just finished the long, difficult, mind-bending slog through George Wolfe's epic four book "New Sun" series.<br/><br/>Warning: this review contains mild spoilers, mostly because it is impossible to discuss the series seriously without them. That said, if you intend to read the books, the spoilers will not actually spoil anything (I think). It is dense enough that even if you know the ENTIRE plot, and have read the denouement several t</span>
  <span id="freeText11584215065973985249" style="display:none">At the encouragement of a good friend of mine who has similar tastes, I just finished the long, difficult, mind-bending slog through George Wolfe's epic four book "New Sun" series.<br /><br />Warning: this review contains mild spoilers, mostly because it is impossible to discuss the series seriously without them. That said, if you intend to read the books, the spoilers will not actually spoil anything (I think). It is dense enough that even if you know the ENTIRE plot, and have read the denouement several times over, there are still many many layers of things to discover and marvel at.<br /><br />So. Here we go.<br /><br />The "Book of the New Sun" series is a lengthy, complex, layered, almost biblical account of a torturer living in a far, far, far flung future who becomes elevated to a planetary ruler and putative savior of Humanity.<br /><br />It is filled with some of my favorite literary devices (no doubt the reason my friend recommended it to me in the first place)<br /><br />* Allegory<br />* Unreliable narration<br />* Blurring of fantasy/reality<br />* Ambiguity (of many kinds: ontological, notional, moral)<br />* Social commentary/satire<br />* Stories within stories (flash backs, embedded morality tales, comic relief, meta-commentary, etc.)<br />* Neologisms galore (many tailored to linguistic wonk types)<br /><br />And last but not least<br /><br />* An interesting treatment of time travel (more aligned along the lines of the brilliant Bill and Ted's approach, rather that of the moronic Back to the Future).<br /><br />Or, if you've read it, the innovative "Gates of Anubis" take on time travel...<br /><br />Above all, it is a book about religion (in a metaphysical/philosophical sense, not the literal sense), morality and redemption. The first person narrator (Severian) is a professional torturer, raised in a guild of torturers to faithfully execute (sometimes literally), to the letter, the sentences determined by the functionaries of the Commonwealth (ostensibly ruled by the Autarch). However, his planet (Urth) is embroiled in constant and violent conflict, as there are several factions that seek to end the Autarch's presumably despotic and inflexible rule. To make matters worse, Urth's sun is dying, and there are signs of it fading from existence entirely. Not only have technological advances been arrested to a standstill (by mechanisms unspecified), the resources of the planet are all but exhausted, since by all accounts, the history of Humanity on Urth is a very very ancient one. The only hope is the arrival (or delivery? installation?) of the "New Sun", a savior(?) who will presumably restore the dying sun's function, uplift the Human race, and restore it to its spacefaring capabilities of populating the galaxy, if not the universe(s), with its people and culture.<br /><br />The writing style is not that of a typical genre book; rather, it reads more like the memoirs of a prophet, and, as I've said, carries a distinctly biblical tone. The many layered prose is very dense, and the language full of neologisms (perhaps more accurately retrologisms) whose definitions are left for the reader to determine. Much like the brilliant opening of Stephenson's "Anathem", however, clear context and linguistic hints are in ample supply, and doing the decoding is fun and always enlightening, since language forms an integral part of the themes of the series.<br /><br />Severian himself is a complex character, and while his approach to narration is often excruciatingly detached and literal, it belies a much more complex truth; that of the delicate, ambiguous relationship between memory, reality, and identity. The writing is manifold; multiple (conflicting) meanings pervade almost every paragraph, and each and every scene carries with it the feeling that there are things unsaid and unexpressed that will not be fully explained until later. Even as the dialog can be (paradoxically) expositional in nature, and no matter how much information is explicitly told (not shown), there is always yet another layer of "show, don't tell" hidden underneath.<br /><br />The entire series itself is a puzzle to be solved and a riddle to be answered by the reader. Unlike many lazy mystery novels, though, the clues are not deliberately hidden from view, or omitted by the author to artificially provide challenge; even Severian himself tells you from the start that everything there is to be told is right there in the telling. The reader need only to identify and synthesize.<br /><br />In and of itself, this challenge makes the read a compelling one, but the overall plot is sufficient incentive to keep reading even if the reader's puzzle solving drive wanes. The characters are believable, varied and interesting, and there is comic relief and enough self-awareness to keep the tone from being overly oppressive and smug. That said, there are some weak characterizations; in particular, the narrator's relationship with women seems overly sexualized and two dimensional. However, that, and the fact that many of the female characters are correspondingly shallow, can be excused in context - the narrator is consistently portrayed as somewhat naive, if not outright flawed, and often blindingly self-involved.<br /><br />I also had trouble with the overarching message; that of the necessity of punishing humanity, not just from a practical standpoint (as steel being forged, or a puppy being trained), but as a moral imperative to find the "truth" of the nature and value of humanity. It comes as no surprise then, that the author is a devout Catholic, and perhaps that is just my strong distaste for organized religion showing. Why should redemption or a test of value require punishment? If you are a trans-human, hyper advanced alien, or notional deity, either reward, or do not reward. Certainly the carrot is sufficient - why the stick? Does not the level of inaction required to keep the denizens of Urth in this particular state of misery to ostensibly produce redemption rise to the standard of immoral (or at least unethical) action? Especially if that inaction comes from a galactic (or trans-universal) culture capable of re-igniting a sun, not to mention FTL and time travel.<br /><br />However, it does work in the context of the book itself, and setting aside my personal (probably unfair) bias, the text exhibits enough self-awareness of this paradox that I don't think it takes away from the wonder, emotion, and thrust of the series as a whole.<br /><br />If you enjoy books that take more than one reading to extract their full meaning (in this case, probably many more than one), this is a wonderful way to challenge yourself.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11584215065973985249" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2399617088?book_show_action=true">May 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christian Holub" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christian Holub" href="/user/show/2103348-christian-holub">Christian Holub</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2399617088" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18114814511374056061">Like Lord of the Rings, Book of the New Sun is one novel split into smaller volumes collecting smaller books. Just as LOTR was one novel comprised of six books collected into three volumes, Wolfe's magnum opus is one novel of four books collected in two volumes. What this means is that even though this feels like a sequel to Shadow & Claw, they're all part of the same story, and several scenes toward the end of this volume reach back to the very beginning of the first volume. Therefore, to fully</span>
  <span id="freeText18114814511374056061" style="display:none">Like Lord of the Rings, Book of the New Sun is one novel split into smaller volumes collecting smaller books. Just as LOTR was one novel comprised of six books collected into three volumes, Wolfe's magnum opus is one novel of four books collected in two volumes. What this means is that even though this feels like a sequel to Shadow &amp; Claw, they're all part of the same story, and several scenes toward the end of this volume reach back to the very beginning of the first volume. Therefore, to fully appreciate this book, I think I'm going to be turning it over in my head and flipping back and forth for a long time. But what I could understand of this gothic Catholic-influenced fantasy/sci-fi hybrid about war, pain, death, and resurrection was incredible. There are a TON of hard-to-understand words, but as long as you can roll with it they really build up this unique atmosphere that totally swept me away. It's a challenge, but one you feel better for completing. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18114814511374056061" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_304217'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/304217.Viriconium"><img alt="Viriconium" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1347891771l/304217._SX98_.jpg" />
</a>

  
<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('AXENbwJ5UlImVYfvzxKjst8inDapip41uOrw+EBsLJ0kslIMhcewWBoozcz18mEuiHkRYk3SxhF47XqWYxRSMQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('YpaqVLGwcknGt4DkxJAYVVOa0GLSLgVLGbCdq/KFY8RHVfU3Ng6QQ/rKysf+cNrJBMFdNjZ2XW/ZtxfF0f0daA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

The Thirst for Annihilation is the first book in English to respond to Bataille\'s writings. In no way, though, is Nick Land\'s book an attempt to appropriate Bataille\'s writings to a secular intelligibility or to compromise with the aridity of academic discourse - rather, it is written as a communion. Theoretical issues in philosophy, sociology, psychodynamics, politics and poetry are discussed, but only as stepping stones into the deep water of textual sacrifice where words pass over into the broken voice of death. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3374126746311961814">Gene Wolfe was an American science fiction and fantasy writer. He was noted for his dense, allusive prose as well as the strong influence of his Catholic faith, to which he converted after marrying a Catholic. He was a prolific short story writer and a novelist, and has won many awards in the field.<br/><br/>The Damon Knight Memorial Grand Master Award is given by SFWA for ‘lifetime achievement in science f</span>
  <span id="freeText3374126746311961814" style="display:none">Gene Wolfe was an American science fiction and fantasy writer. He was noted for his dense, allusive prose as well as the strong influence of his Catholic faith, to which he converted after marrying a Catholic. He was a prolific short story writer and a novelist, and has won many awards in the field.<br /><br />The Damon Knight Memorial Grand Master Award is given by SFWA for ‘lifetime achievement in science fiction and/or fantasy.’ Wolfe joins the Grand Master ranks alongside such legends as Connie Willis, Michael Moorcock, Anne McCaffrey, Robert Silverberg, Ursula K. Le Guin, Isaac Asimov, Ray Bradbury and Joe Haldeman. The award will be presented at the 48th Annual Nebula Awards Weekend in San Jose, CA, May 16-19, 2013.<br /><br />While attending Texas A&amp;M University Wolfe published his first speculative fiction in The Commentator, a student literary journal. Wolfe dropped out during his junior year, and was drafted to fight in the Korean War. After returning to the United States he earned a degree from the University of Houston and became an industrial engineer. He edited the journal Plant Engineering for many years before retiring to write full-time, but his most famous professional engineering achievement is a contribution to the machine used to make Pringles potato crisps. He lived in Barrington, Illinois, a suburb of Chicago.<br /><br />A frequent <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/award/show/9-hugo-award" rel="nofollow noopener">Hugo</a> nominee without a win, Wolfe has nevertheless picked up several <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/award/show/23-nebula-award" rel="nofollow noopener">Nebula</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/award/show/46-locus-award" rel="nofollow noopener">Locus</a> Awards, among others, including the World Fantasy Award for Life Achievement and the 2012 Damon Knight Memorial Grand Master Award. He is also a member of the Science Fiction Hall of Fame.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://us.macmillan.com/author/genewolfe">http://us.macmillan.com/author/genewolfe</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="3374126746311961814" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
